1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B5._%D0%91%D1%8E%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%90.%D0%9F._%D0%93%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B1%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/440px-%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B5._%D0%91%D1%8E%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%90.%D0%9F._%D0%93%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B1%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2446" data-file-height="3669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B5._%D0%91%D1%8E%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%90.%D0%9F._%D0%93%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B1%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Russian general <a href="/wiki/Abram_Gannibal" class="mw-redirect" title="Abram Gannibal">Abram Gannibal</a>, who was the great-grandfather of Russia's greatest poet <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pushkin" title="Alexander Pushkin">Alexander Pushkin</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg/170px-Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg/255px-Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg/340px-Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnaldo_Tamayo_Berlin_2018_-_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arnaldo_Tamayo_M%C3%A9ndez" title="Arnaldo Tamayo Méndez">Arnaldo Tamayo Méndez</a> is a former <a href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonaut</a> and the first person of African heritage in space.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 1978 he was selected to join the <a href="/wiki/Intercosmos" class="mw-redirect" title="Intercosmos">Intercosmos</a> program and moved to <a href="/wiki/Star_City,_Russia" title="Star City, Russia">Star City</a> in Russia for his training as a cosmonaut.</div></div></div>
<p>As African states <a href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonization of Africa">became independent</a> in the 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> offered many of their citizens the chance to study in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Over a period of 40 years, about 400,000 African students from various countries moved to Russia to pursue higher studies, including many Black Africans.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This extended beyond the Soviet Union to many countries of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkans">Balkans</span></h4>
<p>Due to the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Slavery in the Ottoman Empire">slave trade</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> that had flourished in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the coastal town of <a href="/wiki/Ulcinj" title="Ulcinj">Ulcinj</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> had its own black community.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> As a consequence of the slave trade and <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateer</a> activity, it is told how until 1878 in Ulcinj 100 black people lived.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Military_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Military of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Army</a> also deployed an estimated 30,000 Black African troops and cavalrymen to its expedition in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Turkish_War_of_1716%E2%80%9318" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Turkish War of 1716–18">Austro-Turkish War of 1716–18</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/African_Australians" title="African Australians">African Australians</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_New_Zealanders" title="African New Zealanders">African New Zealanders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_Australians">Indigenous Australians</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:136px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aborigine.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Aborigine.png/134px-Aborigine.png" decoding="async" width="134" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Aborigine.png/201px-Aborigine.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Aborigine.png/268px-Aborigine.png 2x" data-file-width="340" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aborigine.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unknown <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Aboriginal</a> woman in 1911</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> have been referred to as "black people" in Australia since the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia_(1788%E2%80%931850)" title="History of Australia (1788–1850)">early days of European settlement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> While originally related to <a href="/wiki/Skin_colour" class="mw-redirect" title="Skin colour">skin colour</a>, the term is used today to indicate Aboriginal or <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait_Islanders" title="Torres Strait Islanders">Torres Strait Islander</a> ancestry in general and can refer to people of any skin pigmentation.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Being identified as either "black" or "white" in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> during the 19th and early 20th centuries was critical in one's employment and social prospects. Various state-based <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Protection_Board" title="Aboriginal Protection Board">Aboriginal Protection Boards</a> were established which had virtually complete control over the lives of Indigenous Australians – where they lived, their employment, marriage, education and included the power to separate children from their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Aborigines were not allowed to vote and were often confined to reserves and forced into low paid or effectively slave labour.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The social position of mixed-race or "<a href="/wiki/Half-caste" title="Half-caste">half-caste</a>" individuals varied over time. A 1913 report by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baldwin_Spencer" title="Walter Baldwin Spencer">Baldwin Spencer</a> states that:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the half-castes belong neither to the aboriginal nor to the whites, yet, on the whole, they have more leaning towards the former; ... One thing is certain and that is that the white population as a whole will never mix with half-castes... the best and kindest thing is to place them on reserves along with the natives, train them in the same schools and encourage them to marry amongst themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, however, it became apparent that the number of mixed-race people was growing at a faster rate than the white population, and by 1930 fear of the "half-caste menace" undermining the <a href="/wiki/White_Australia_policy" title="White Australia policy">White Australia</a> ideal from within was being taken as a serious concern.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Cook_(Australia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cecil Cook (Australia)">Cecil Cook</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> <a href="/wiki/Protector_of_Aborigines" title="Protector of Aborigines">Protector of Natives</a>, noted that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>generally by the fifth and invariably by the sixth generation, all native characteristics of the Australian Aborigine are eradicated. The problem of our half-castes will quickly be eliminated by the complete disappearance of the black race, and the swift submergence of their progeny in the white.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The official policy became one of biological and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">cultural assimilation</a>: "Eliminate the full-blood and permit the white admixture to half-castes and eventually the race will become white".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> This led to different treatment for "black" and "half-caste" individuals, with lighter-skinned individuals targeted for removal from their families to be raised as "white" people, restricted from speaking their native language and practising traditional customs, a process now known as the <a href="/wiki/Stolen_Generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Stolen Generation">Stolen Generation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally,_Jan_26_2007,_Brisbane,_Queensland,_Australia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg/150px-Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg/225px-Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg/300px-Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally%2C_Jan_26_2007%2C_Brisbane%2C_Queensland%2C_Australia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="396" data-file-height="740" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sam_Watson_Addresses_Invasion_Day_Rally,_Jan_26_2007,_Brisbane,_Queensland,_Australia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Aboriginal</a> <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activist</a> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Watson_(activist)" title="Sam Watson (activist)">Sam Watson</a> addressing Invasion Day Rally 2007 in a "Australia has a Black History" T-shirt</div></div></div>
<p>The second half of the 20th century to the present has seen a gradual shift towards improved human rights for Aboriginal people. In <a href="/wiki/Australian_referendum,_1967_(Aboriginals)" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian referendum, 1967 (Aboriginals)">a 1967 referendum</a> over 90% of the Australian population voted to end constitutional discrimination and to include Aborigines in the national <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> During this period many Aboriginal activists began to embrace the term "black" and use their ancestry as a source of pride. Activist <a href="/wiki/Bob_Maza" title="Bob Maza">Bob Maza</a> said:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I only hope that when I die I can say I'm black and it's beautiful to be black. It is this sense of pride which we are trying to give back to the aborigine [<i>sic</i>] today.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In 1978 Aboriginal writer <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Gilbert_(author)" title="Kevin Gilbert (author)">Kevin Gilbert</a> received the National Book Council award for his book <i>Living Black: Blacks Talk to Kevin Gilbert</i>, a collection of Aboriginal people's stories, and in 1998 was awarded (but refused to accept) the Human Rights Award for Literature for <i>Inside Black Australia</i>, a poetry anthology and exhibition of Aboriginal photography.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to previous definitions based solely on the degree of Aboriginal ancestry, in 1990 the Government changed the legal definition of Aboriginal to include any:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>person of Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander descent who identifies as an Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander and is accepted as such by the community in which he [or she] lives<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Multicultural_Festival_2012_(6850952247).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg/170px-Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg/255px-Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg/340px-Multicultural_Festival_2012_%286850952247%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Multicultural_Festival_2012_(6850952247).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigerian</a> attendants at the 2012 <a href="/wiki/National_Multicultural_Festival" title="National Multicultural Festival">National Multicultural Festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a></div></div></div>
<p>This nationwide acceptance and recognition of Aboriginal people led to a significant increase in the number of people self-identifying as Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reappropriation" title="Reappropriation">reappropriation</a> of the term "black" with a positive and more inclusive meaning has resulted in its widespread use in mainstream Australian culture, including public media outlets,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> government agencies,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and private companies.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, a number of high-profile cases highlighted the legal and community attitude that identifying as Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander is not dependent on skin colour, with a well-known boxer <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Mundine" title="Anthony Mundine">Anthony Mundine</a> being widely criticised for questioning the "blackness" of another boxer<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and journalist <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Bolt" title="Andrew Bolt">Andrew Bolt</a> being successfully sued for publishing discriminatory comments about Aboriginals with light skin.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Caesar" title="John Caesar">John Caesar</a>, nicknamed "Black Caesar", a <a href="/wiki/Convicts_in_Australia" title="Convicts in Australia">convict</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bushranger" title="Bushranger">bushranger</a> with parents born in an unknown area in Africa, was one of the first people of recent Black African ancestry to arrive in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the 2006 Census, 248,605 residents declared that they were <a href="/wiki/African_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="African Australian">born in Africa</a>. This figure pertains to all immigrants to Australia who were born in nations in Africa regardless of race, and includes <a href="/wiki/White_Africans_of_European_ancestry" title="White Africans of European ancestry">White Africans</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Black_Canadians" title="Black Canadians">Black Canadians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anderson-Ruffin-Abbott.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Anderson-Ruffin-Abbott.jpg/170px-Anderson-Ruffin-Abbott.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Anderson-Ruffin-Abbott.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="238" data-file-height="346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anderson-Ruffin-Abbott.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anderson_Ruffin_Abbott" title="Anderson Ruffin Abbott">Anderson Ruffin Abbott</a>, the first Black Canadian to be a licensed physician, participated in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and attended the deathbed of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Black Canadians is a designation used for people of Black African ancestry, who are citizens or permanent residents of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Canada&#39;s_Peoples_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Canada&#39;s_Peoples-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The majority of Black Canadians are of <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> origin, though the population also consists of <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> immigrants and their descendants (including <a href="/wiki/Black_Nova_Scotians" title="Black Nova Scotians">Black Nova Scotians</a>), as well as many <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-canada_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canada-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Black Canadians often draw a distinction between those of <a href="/wiki/Afro-Caribbean" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Caribbean">Afro-Caribbean</a> ancestry and those of other African roots. The term <i>African Canadian</i> is occasionally used by some Black Canadians who trace their heritage to the first slaves brought by British and French colonists to the North American mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Canada&#39;s_Peoples_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Canada&#39;s_Peoples-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Promised freedom by the British during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, thousands of <a href="/wiki/Black_Loyalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Loyalists">Black Loyalists</a> were resettled by the Crown in Canada afterward, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Peters_(revolutionary)" title="Thomas Peters (revolutionary)">Thomas Peters</a>. In addition, an estimated ten to thirty thousand <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_slaves_in_the_United_States" title="Fugitive slaves in the United States">fugitive slaves</a> reached freedom in Canada from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a> during the antebellum years, aided by people along the <a href="/wiki/Underground_Railroad" title="Underground Railroad">Underground Railroad</a>.
</p><p>Many Black people of Caribbean origin in Canada reject the term African Canadian as an elision of the uniquely Caribbean aspects of their heritage,<sup id="cite_ref-walcott_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walcott-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and instead identify as <i>Caribbean Canadian</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-walcott_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walcott-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Unlike in the United States, where African American has become a widely used term, in Canada controversies associated with distinguishing African or Caribbean heritage have resulted in the term "Black Canadian" being widely accepted there.<sup id="cite_ref-pruegger_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pruegger-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/170px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/255px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg/340px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2473" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_NYWTS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Civil rights activist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King">Martin Luther King</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>There were eight principal areas used by Europeans to buy and ship slaves to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>. The number of enslaved people sold to the New World varied throughout the slave trade. As for the distribution of slaves from regions of activity, certain areas produced far more enslaved people than others. Between 1650 and 1900, 10.24 million enslaved West Africans arrived in the Americas from the following regions in the following proportions:<sup id="cite_ref-Lovejoy_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovejoy-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Senegambia_(geography)" class="mw-redirect" title="Senegambia (geography)">Senegambia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">the Gambia</a>): 4.8%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Upper_Guinea" title="Upper Guinea">Upper Guinea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>): 4.1%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pepper_Coast" title="Pepper Coast">Windward Coast</a> (<a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>): 1.8%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast_(British_colony)" title="Gold Coast (British colony)">Gold Coast</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and east of <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>): 10.4%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Benin" title="Bight of Benin">Bight of Benin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> west of the Niger Delta): 20.2%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Biafra" title="Bight of Biafra">Bight of Biafra</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> east of the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>): 14.6%</li>
<li>West Central Africa (<a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Congo">Republic of Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>): 39.4%</li>
<li>Southeastern Africa (<a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>): 4.7%</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_(8928374600).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg/170px-Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg/255px-Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg/340px-Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_%288928374600%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4520" data-file-height="3235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_trade_from_Africa_to_the_Americas_(8928374600).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main slave routes in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Slave_Trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Slave Trade">Atlantic Slave Trade</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The variants <i>neger</i> and <i>negar</i>, derive from the Spanish and Portuguese word <i lang="es" title="Spanish language text">negro</i> (black), and from the now-pejorative French <i>nègre</i> (negro). Etymologically, <i>negro</i>, <i>noir</i>, <i>nègre</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Nigger" title="Nigger">nigger</a></i> ultimately derive from <i>nigrum</i>, the stem of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">niger</i> (black) (pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈniɡer]</span> which, in every other <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">grammatical case</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical gender</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">grammatical number</a> besides <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> masculine singular, is <i>nigr-</i>, the <i>r</i> is <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar trill">trilled</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SojournerTruth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/SojournerTruth.jpg/170px-SojournerTruth.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/SojournerTruth.jpg/255px-SojournerTruth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/SojournerTruth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SojournerTruth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>African American justice activist <a href="/wiki/Sojourner_Truth" title="Sojourner Truth">Sojourner Truth</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg/170px-Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg/255px-Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg/340px-Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5400" data-file-height="8651" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harriet_Tubman_c1868-69.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a>, an <a href="/wiki/African-American" class="mw-redirect" title="African-American">African-American</a> fugitive slave, <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a>, and conductor of the <a href="/wiki/Underground_Railroad" title="Underground Railroad">Underground Railroad</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the 1900s, <i>nigger</i> had become a pejorative word in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. In its stead, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Colored" title="Colored">colored</a></i> became the mainstream alternative to <i><a href="/wiki/Negro" title="Negro">negro</a></i> and its derived terms. After the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a>, the terms <i>colored</i> and <i>negro</i> gave way to "black". <i>Negro</i> had superseded <i>colored</i> as the most polite word for <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> at a time when <i>black</i> was considered more offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This term was accepted as normal, including by people classified as Negroes, until the later <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights">Civil Rights</a> movement in the late 1960s. One well-known example is the identification by Reverend <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> of his own race as "Negro" in his famous speech of 1963, <a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>. During the American civil rights movement of the 1950s and 1960s, some African-American leaders in the United States, notably <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_X" title="Malcolm X">Malcolm X</a>, objected to the word <i>Negro</i> because they associated it with the long history of slavery, segregation, and discrimination that treated African Americans as second-class citizens, or worse.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Malcolm X preferred <i>Black</i> to <i>Negro</i>, but later gradually abandoned that as well for <i>Afro-American</i> after leaving the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the late 1960s, various other terms for African Americans have been more widespread in popular usage. Aside from <i>Black American</i>, these include <i>Afro-American</i> (in use from the late 1960s to 1990) and <i>African American</i> (used in the United States to refer to Black Americans, people often referred to in the past as <i>American Negroes</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg/170px-Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg/255px-Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg/340px-Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3738" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_restored.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong" title="Louis Armstrong">Louis Armstrong</a> was an American jazz trumpeter, composer, singer and actor.</div></div></div>
<p>In the first 200 years that black people were in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, they primarily identified themselves by their specific <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">ethnic group</a> (closely allied to language) and not by skin color. Individuals identified themselves, for example, as <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_people" title="Ashanti people">Ashanti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">Bakongo</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wolof_people" title="Wolof people">Wolof</a>. However, when the first captives were brought to <a href="/wiki/The_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="The Americas">the Americas</a>, they were often combined with other groups from West Africa, and individual ethnic affiliations were not generally acknowledged by English colonists. In areas of the Upper South, different ethnic groups were brought together. This is significant as the captives came from a vast geographic region: the West African coastline stretching from <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> and in some cases from the south-east coast such as <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>. A new <i>African-American</i> identity and culture was born that incorporated elements of the various ethnic groups and of European cultural heritage, resulting in fusions such as the <a href="/wiki/Black_church" title="Black church">Black church</a> and <a href="/wiki/African-American_English" title="African-American English">African-American English</a>. This new identity was based on provenance and slave status rather than membership in any one ethnic group.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, slave records from Louisiana show that the French and Spanish colonists recorded more complete identities of the West Africans, including ethnicities and given tribal names.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The US racial or ethnic classification "black" refers to people with all possible kinds of skin pigmentation, from the darkest through to the very lightest skin colors, including <a href="/wiki/Albinos" class="mw-redirect" title="Albinos">albinos</a>, if they are believed by others to have African ancestry (in any discernible percentage). There are also certain cultural traits associated with being "<a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a>", a term used effectively as a synonym for "black person" within the United States.
</p><p>In March 1807, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Great Britain</a>, which largely controlled the Atlantic, declared <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_1807" title="Slave Trade Act 1807">the transatlantic slave trade illegal</a>, as did the United States. (The latter prohibition took effect 1 January 1808, the earliest date on which <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> had the power to do so after protecting the slave trade under <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States_of_America#Section_9" class="extiw" title="s:Constitution of the United States of America">Article I, Section 9</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>.)
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg/170px-Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg/255px-Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg/340px-Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarence_Thomas_official_SCOTUS_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States</a> <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Thomas" title="Clarence Thomas">Clarence Thomas</a></div></div></div>
<p>By that time, the majority of black people in the United States were native-born, so the use of the term "African" became problematic. Though initially a source of pride, many blacks feared that the use of African as an identity would be a hindrance to their fight for full citizenship in the US. They also felt that it would give ammunition to those who were advocating repatriating black people back to Africa. In 1835, black leaders called upon Black Americans to remove the title of "African" from their institutions and replace it with "<a href="/wiki/Negro" title="Negro">Negro</a>" or "Colored American". A few institutions chose to keep their historic names, such as the <a href="/wiki/African_Methodist_Episcopal_Church" title="African Methodist Episcopal Church">African Methodist Episcopal Church</a>. African Americans popularly used the terms "Negro" or "colored" for themselves until the late 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>black</i> was used throughout but not frequently since it carried a certain stigma. In his 1963 "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> uses the terms <i>negro</i> fifteen times and <i>black</i> four times. Each time he uses <i>black</i> it is in parallel construction with <i>white</i>; for example, "black men and white men".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the successes of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a>, a new term was needed to break from the past and help shed the reminders of legalized discrimination. In place of <i>Negro</i>, activists promoted the use of <i>black</i> as standing for racial pride, militancy, and power. Some of the turning points included the use of the term "<a href="/wiki/Black_Power" title="Black Power">Black Power</a>" by Kwame Toure (<a href="/wiki/Stokely_Carmichael" title="Stokely Carmichael">Stokely Carmichael</a>) and the popular singer <a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Say_It_Loud_%E2%80%93_I%27m_Black_and_I%27m_Proud" title="Say It Loud – I&#39;m Black and I&#39;m Proud">Say It Loud – I'm Black and I'm Proud</a>".
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/170px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/255px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg 2x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a>, an African American basketball player.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1988, the civil rights leader <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a> urged Americans to use instead the term "African American" because it had a historical cultural base and was a construction similar to terms used by European descendants, such as German American, Italian American, etc. Since then, African American and black have often had parallel status. However, controversy continues over which if any of the two terms is more appropriate. <a href="/wiki/Maulana_Karenga" title="Maulana Karenga">Maulana Karenga</a> argues that the term African-American is more appropriate because it accurately articulates their geographical and historical origin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Others have argued that "black" is a better term because "African" suggests foreignness, although Black Americans helped found the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Still others believe that the term black is inaccurate because African Americans have a variety of skin tones.<sup id="cite_ref-Relethford2000_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Relethford2000-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shriver2003_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shriver2003-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Some surveys suggest that the majority of Black Americans have no preference for "African American" or "Black",<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> although they have a slight preference for "black" in personal settings and "African American" in more formal settings.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (United States Census)">U.S. census race definitions</a>, Black and African Americans are citizens and residents of the United States with origins in Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-IOM_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Office of Management and Budget">Office of Management and Budget</a>, the grouping includes individuals who self-identify as African American, as well as persons who emigrated from nations in the Caribbean and Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-2010USCBAA_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010USCBAA-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The grouping is thus based on geography, and may contradict or misrepresent an individual's self-identification since not all immigrants from Sub-Saharan Africa are "Black".<sup id="cite_ref-IOM_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The Census Bureau also notes that these classifications are socio-political constructs and should not be interpreted as scientific or anthropological.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to US Census Bureau data, <a href="/wiki/African_immigration_to_the_United_States" title="African immigration to the United States">African immigrants</a> generally do not self-identify as African American. The overwhelming majority of African immigrants identify instead with their own respective ethnicities (~95%).<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Immigrants from some <a href="/wiki/Afro-Caribbean" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afro-Latin_Americans" title="Afro-Latin Americans">Central American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afro-Latin_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Latin American">South American</a> nations and their descendants may or may not also self-identify with the term.<sup id="cite_ref-LewisM_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LewisM-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent surveys of African Americans using a genetic testing service have found varied ancestries which show different tendencies by region and sex of ancestors. These studies found that on average, African Americans have 73.2–80.9% <a href="/wiki/West_African_people" class="mw-redirect" title="West African people">West African</a>, 18–24% European, and 0.8–0.9% <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> genetic heritage, with large variation between individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Bryc2009_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bryc2009-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bryc_2015_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bryc_2015-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="One-drop_rule">One-drop rule</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Douglass_(circa_1879).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg/170px-Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg/255px-Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg/340px-Frederick_Douglass_%28circa_1879%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2089" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Douglass_(circa_1879).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multiracial social reformer <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>From the late 19th century, the South used a <a href="/wiki/Colloquial" class="mw-redirect" title="Colloquial">colloquial</a> term, the <i><a href="/wiki/One-drop_rule" title="One-drop rule">one-drop rule</a></i>, to classify as black a person of any known African ancestry. This practice of hypodescent was not put into law until the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Davis_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Legally the definition varied from state to state. Racial definition was more flexible in the 18th and 19th centuries before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. For instance, President <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> held persons who were legally white (less than 25% black) according to Virginia law at the time, but, because they were born to slave mothers, they were born into slavery, according to the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Partus_sequitur_ventrem" title="Partus sequitur ventrem">partus sequitur ventrem</a></i>, which Virginia adopted into law in 1662.
</p><p>Outside the U.S., some other countries have adopted the one-drop rule, but the definition of who is black and the extent to which the one-drop "rule" applies varies greatly from country to country.
</p><p>The one-drop rule may have originated as a means of increasing the number of black slaves<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and was maintained as an attempt to keep the white race pure.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag relies on an unreliable source. Frank Sweet is not a reliable source. His is a self-published fringe viewpoint. (September 2017)">unreliable source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> One of the results of the one-drop rule was the uniting of the African-American community.<sup id="cite_ref-Davis_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Some of the most prominent abolitionists and civil-rights activists of the 19th century were multiracial, such as <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Purvis" title="Robert Purvis">Robert Purvis</a> and James Mercer Langston. They advocated equality for all.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blackness">Blackness</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg/170px-Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg/255px-Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg/340px-Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1916" data-file-height="2608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Official_portrait_of_Barack_Obama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> — the first person of color, biracial, and self-identified African American <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> — was throughout <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama_2008_presidential_campaign" title="Barack Obama 2008 presidential campaign">his campaign</a> criticized as being either "too black" or "not black enough".<sup id="cite_ref-obama-speech_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obama-speech-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-obama-time_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obama-time-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The concept of blackness in the United States has been described as the degree to which one associates themselves with mainstream <a href="/wiki/African-American_culture" title="African-American culture">African-American culture</a>, politics,<sup id="cite_ref-Eze_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olson_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and values.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> To a certain extent, this concept is not so much about race but more about political orientation,<sup id="cite_ref-Eze_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olson_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> culture and behavior. Blackness can be contrasted with "<a href="/wiki/Acting_white" title="Acting white">acting white</a>", where black Americans are said to behave with assumed characteristics of stereotypical white Americans with regard to <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a>, taste in <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> and possibly, from the perspective of a significant number of black youth, academic achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the often political<sup id="cite_ref-Eze_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olson_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and cultural contours of blackness in the United States, the notion of blackness can also be extended to non-black people. <a href="/wiki/Toni_Morrison" title="Toni Morrison">Toni Morrison</a> once described <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> as the first black <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> because, as she put it, he displayed "almost every trope of blackness".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> was offended by the notion of Clinton as the first black president, noting, "Mr Clinton, according to Toni Morrison, the Nobel Prize-winning novelist, is our first black President, the first to come from the broken home, the alcoholic mother, the under-the-bridge shadows of our ranking systems. Thus, we may have lost the mystical power to divine diabolism, but we can still divine blackness by the following symptoms: broken homes, alcoholic mothers, under-the-bridge habits and (presumable from the rest of [Arthur] Miller's senescent musings) the tendency to sexual predation and to shameless perjury about same."<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Some black activists were also offended, claiming that Clinton used his knowledge of black culture to exploit black people for political gain as no other president had before, while not serving black interests.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> They cite the lack of action during the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and his <a href="/wiki/Welfare_reform" title="Welfare reform">welfare reform</a>, which Larry Roberts said had led to the worst <a href="/wiki/Child_poverty" title="Child poverty">child poverty</a> since the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Others cited that the number of black people in jail increased during his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The question of blackness also arose in the Democrat <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">2008 presidential campaign</a>. Commentators have questioned whether Obama, who was elected the first president with black ancestry, is "black enough", contending that his background is not typical because his mother was <a href="/wiki/White_American" class="mw-redirect" title="White American">white American</a>, and his father was a black <a href="/wiki/Kenyan_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenyan American">Kenyan immigrant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-obama-speech_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obama-speech-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-obama-time_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obama-time-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Obama chose to identify as black and <a href="/wiki/African-American" class="mw-redirect" title="African-American">African-American</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kroft_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kroft-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2012, Ancestry.com reported on historic and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> research by its staff that discovered that Obama is likely a descendant through his mother of <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a>, considered by some historians to be the first African slave in the <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> colony. An <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured servant">indentured servant</a>, he was "bound for life" in 1640 after trying to escape. The story of him and his descendants is that of multi-racial America since it appeared he and his sons married or had unions with white women, likely indentured servants and working-class like them. Their <a href="/wiki/Multi-racial" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-racial">multi-racial</a> children were free because they were born to free <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a> women. Over time, Obama's line of the Bunch family (as they became known) were property owners and continued to "marry white"; they became part of white society, likely by the early to mid-18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Harman_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harman-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_mixing_in_the_United_States">Race mixing in the United States</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Race_mixing" class="mw-redirect" title="Race mixing">Race mixing</a> in the United States took place between <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free colored people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Americans" title="European Americans">European Americans</a>. In Virginia, negro former slaves who had maintained their freedom married Native American women.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Mexicans" title="Afro-Mexicans">Afro-Mexicans</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people#Spanish_colonial_caste_system" title="Criollo people">Society and black people in the Spanish Colonial Americas</a></div>
<p>200,000 slaves were shipped from West Africa to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> by Spanish conquistadors. The history of these Afro-Mexicans was hidden until 2016. Racism against them and dark skin generally in Mexico and most of Latin America is prevalent.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people#Spanish_colonial_caste_system" title="Criollo people">Society and black people in the Spanish Colonial Americas</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominican_Republic">Dominican Republic</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Dominicans" title="Afro-Dominicans">Afro-Dominicans</a></div>
<p>The first Afro-Dominican slaves were shipped to the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> by Spanish conquistadors during the Transatlantic slave trade.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Puerto_Rico">Puerto Rico</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Puerto_Ricans" title="Afro-Puerto Ricans">Afro-Puerto Ricans</a></div>
<p>Spanish conquistadors shipped slaves from West Africa to <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>. Afro-Puerto Ricans in part trace descent to this colonization of the island.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Cuban" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Cuban">Afro-Cuban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afro-Guatemalan" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Guatemalan">Afro-Guatemalan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afro-Hondurans" title="Afro-Hondurans">Afro-Hondurans</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people#Spanish_colonial_caste_system" title="Criollo people">Society and black people in the Spanish Colonial Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rugendasroda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Rugendasroda.jpg/250px-Rugendasroda.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Rugendasroda.jpg/375px-Rugendasroda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Rugendasroda.jpg/500px-Rugendasroda.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1108" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rugendasroda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Capoeira" title="Capoeira">Capoeira</a>, an Afro-Brazilian martial art.</div></div></div>
<p>Approximately 12 million people were shipped from Africa to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> from 1492 to 1888. Of these, 11.5 million of those shipped to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Brazil was the largest importer in the Americas, with 5.5 million African slaves imported, followed by the British Caribbean with 2.76 million, the Spanish Caribbean and Spanish Mainland with 1.59 million Africans, and the French Caribbean with 1.32 million.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Today their descendants number approximately 150 million in South America and the Caribbean.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In addition to skin color, other physical characteristics such as facial features and hair texture are often variously used in classifying peoples as black in South America and the Caribbean.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whitten1998_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitten1998-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In South America and the Caribbean, classification as black is also closely tied to social status and socioeconomic variables, especially in light of social conceptions of "<a href="/wiki/Blanqueamiento" title="Blanqueamiento">blanqueamiento</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Racial_whitening" title="Racial whitening">racial whitening</a>) and related concepts.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitten1998_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitten1998-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afro-Brazilians" title="Afro-Brazilians">Afro-Brazilians</a></div>
<p>The concept of race in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> is complex. A Brazilian child was never automatically identified with the racial type of one or both of his or her parents, nor were there only two categories to choose from. Between an individual of unmixed West African ancestry and a very light <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">mulatto</a> individual, more than a dozen racial categories were acknowledged, based on various combinations of <a href="/wiki/Human_hair_color" title="Human hair color">hair color</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hair#Hair_Texture" title="Hair">hair texture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eye_color" title="Eye color">eye color</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_skin_color" title="Human skin color">skin color</a>. These types grade into each other like the colors of the spectrum, and no one category stands significantly isolated from the rest. In Brazil, people are classified by appearance, not heredity.<sup id="cite_ref-skidmore_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skidmore-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars disagree over the effects of social status on racial classifications in Brazil. It is generally believed that achieving <a href="/wiki/Social_mobility" title="Social mobility">upward mobility</a> and education results in individuals being classified as a category of lighter skin. The popular claim is that in Brazil, poor whites are considered black and wealthy blacks are considered white. Some scholars disagree, arguing that "<a href="/wiki/Racial_whitening" title="Racial whitening">whitening</a>" of one's social status may be open to people of <a href="/wiki/Mixed_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed race">mixed race</a>, a large part of the population known as <i><a href="/wiki/Pardo" title="Pardo">pardo</a></i>, but a person perceived as <i>preto</i> (black) will continue to be classified as black regardless of wealth or social status.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
